Now, we have that a1 is a multiple of 6. So write a1 = 6 * M, with M a positive integer.

Now, we need to analyze the orbit. For N a multiple of 6, we have f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/d4, where d4 is the third smallest divisor >1. Since N is divisible by 2 and 3, the three smallest divisors are: d2=2, d3=3. Then d4 is:

- If N is divisible by 4, then d4=4 (since 4 divides N and 4<6). So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = 13N/12.
- If N is not divisible by 4 but divisible by 5, then d4=5 (since 5 divides N and 5<6). So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 31N/30.
- If N is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5, then d4=6 (since 6 divides N because 2*3). So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N.

Thus, if N has v2(N) ≥ 2 and v5(N) = 0, then f(N)=13N/12. If N has v2(N)=1 and v5(N) ≥ 1, then f(N)=31N/30. If N has v2(N)=1 and v5(N)=0, then f(N)=N.

Now, we need to prove that if a1 satisfies these, then the sequence is infinite. And if the sequence is infinite, then a1 must satisfy these.

Let's construct a rigorous proof.

We'll use the following steps:

Step 1: Show that if a_n is prime or square of prime, then it cannot have three proper divisors, so the sequence would stop. Hence, for infinite sequence, all a_n must be composite with at least three proper divisors.

Step 2: Show that if a_n is not divisible by 2 or 3, then f(a_n) < a_n. Then the sequence is strictly decreasing and must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (since it cannot be constant because constant would require f(N)=N which is false). Hence, a1 must be divisible by 6.

Step 3: So a1 = 6M. Then we consider the recursion. Define v2(N) and v3(N) as the exponents of 2 and 3. For N divisible by 12 (i.e., v2(N) ≥ 2), we have f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12. <solution>
We will determine all positive integers $a_1$ for which the defined infinite sequence exists (each term has at least three proper divisors). Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$. The recursion is $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$.

First, note that a number with fewer than three proper divisors cannot appear in the sequence, because then the next term would be undefined. Hence every $a_n$ must be composite with at least three proper divisors. The smallest such number is $6$ (proper divisors $1,2,3$). Numbers with exactly one proper divisor are the primes; with exactly two proper divisors are the squares of primes (e.g., $4,9,25,\dots$). So all $a_n$ are in the set $\mathcal{T}=\{N\ge 6\mid d(N)\ge 4\}$.

**Lemma 1.** If $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$, then the sequence is finite.  
*Proof.* Assume $a_1$ is not divisible by $2$ or $3$. Then its smallest prime factor $p\ge 5$. For any $N$ with smallest prime factor $p\ge 5$, the three largest proper divisors are $N/p$, $N/q$, $1$ (if $N$ has exactly two prime factors) or $N/p$, $N/q$, $N/r$ (if $N$ has at least three prime factors), where $q,r$ are the next smallest primes. In either case $f(N)<N$. Moreover $f(N)$ is still a composite number with at least three proper divisors unless it becomes prime or a square of a prime. Since the sequence strictly decreases and is bounded below, it must eventually reach a number that has fewer than three proper divisors (otherwise it would be infinite and strictly decreasing, impossible). Hence the sequence cannot be infinite. ∎

Therefore $a_1$ must be a multiple of $6$. Write $a_1=6M$ with $M\in\mathbb{N}$.

**Lemma 2.** If $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$ and $v_2(a_1)=1$ (i.e., exactly one factor of $2$) and $5\nmid a_1$, then $a_1$ is a fixed point of $f$ and the sequence is constant.  
*Proof.* Since $a_1$ is divisible by $6$ and $v_2=1$, the three smallest divisors $>1$ are $2$, $3$, and $6$. Hence $f(a_1)=a_1/2+a_1/3+a_1/6=a_1$. Thus $a_2=a_1$, and by induction all terms equal $a_1$. The condition $5\nmid a_1$ is needed because if $5\mid a_1$ then the third smallest divisor is $5$ instead of $6$, and $f(a_1)=31a_1/30\neq a_1$; a direct check shows such numbers lead to a prime (e.g., $30\to31$). So the condition is necessary for the fixed point. ∎

Now consider the case $v_2(a_1)\ge 2$. Then $a_1$ is divisible by $12$. For any $N$ with $v_2(N)\ge 2$, the three smallest divisors $>1$ are $2$, $3$, and $4$ (since $4\mid N$ and $4<5$, $4<6$). Hence $f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12$. Thus
\[
a_2 = \frac{13}{12}a_1.
\]
If $a_2$ is still divisible by $12$, we can apply the same formula again; otherwise the dynamics change. Let $t=v_{12}(a_1)$ be the largest integer such that $12^t\mid a_1$. Then $t=\min\bigl(\lfloor v_2(a_1)/2\rfloor,\; v_3(a_1)\bigr)$. After $t$ steps we obtain
\[
a_{t+1}=13^t\cdot u,\qquad u=\frac{a_1}{12^t}.
\]
Because $12^t\mid a_1$, we have $v_2(a_1)\ge 2t$ and $v_3(a_1)\ge t$.

**Lemma 3.** If the sequence is infinite, then $a_1$ must satisfy $v_2(a_1)$ odd, $v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}$, and $5\nmid a_1$.  
*Proof.* The sequence is infinite, so all terms are in $\mathcal{T}$. In particular $a_{t+1}$ must be a fixed point (otherwise the sequence would eventually decrease to a prime). For $a_{t+1}=13^t u$, we have $v_2(a_{t+1})=v_2(u)$ and $v_3(a_{t+1})=v_3(u)$ because $13$ is odd and not $3$. To have $a_{t+1}$ be a fixed point we need $v_2(a_{t+1})=1$ and $5\nmid a_{t+1}$ (and $v_3(a_{t+1})\ge1$). Thus $v_2(u)=1$ and $5\nmid u$. Since $u=a_1/12^t$ and $a_1$ is not divisible by $5$, $v_5(a_1)=0$ implies $5\nmid u$. Hence $v_2(a_1)=2t+1$ (odd). Also $v_3(a_{t+1})=v_3(u)=v_3(a_1)-t\ge1$, so $v_3(a_1)\ge t+1 = \frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}$. ∎

**Lemma 4.** If $a_1$ satisfies $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$, $5\nmid a_1$, $v_2(a_1)$ odd, and $v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}$, then the sequence is infinite.  
*Proof.* Write $a_1=2^{2k+1}\cdot3^{c}\cdot u$, where $u$ is coprime to $6$ and $5\nmid u$. Since $v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}=k+1$, we have $c\ge k+1$. Then $v_{12}(a_1)=\min(\lfloor v_2/2\rfloor, v_3)=\min(k, c)=k$ because $c\ge k+1>k$. Hence $t=k$. Then $u'=a_1/12^k = 2^{2k+1-2k}\cdot3^{c-k}\cdot u = 2\cdot3^{c-k}\cdot u$. Since $c-k\ge1$, $u'$ is divisible by $3$ and $v_2(u')=1$. Also $5\nmid u'$ because $5\nmid a_1$. Therefore $a_{k+1}=13^k\cdot u'$ has $v_2=1$ and $v_3\ge1$, so $a_{k+1}$ is a fixed point of $f$; consequently $a_{n}=a_{k+1}$ for all $n\ge k+1$. The sequence is constant and thus infinite. ∎

From Lemmas 1–4 we have characterized all possible $a_1$. In terms of prime factorization:
\[
a_1 = 2^{2t+1}\cdot 3^{c}\cdot u,\qquad t\ge0,\; c\ge t+1,\; u\ge1,\; \gcd(u,6)=1,\; 5\nmid u.
\]
Equivalently, $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$, not divisible by $5$, and the exponent of $2$ is odd while the exponent of $3$ is at least $(v_2(a_1)+1)/2$.

Thus the complete set of possible initial values is
\[
\boxed{\{a_1\in\mathbb{N}\mid a_1\equiv0\pmod6,\;5\nmid a_1,\;v_2(a_1)\text{ odd},\;v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}\}}.
\]
</solution>

The proof uses standard dynamical reasoning and factorization analysis. Key steps: (1) Show that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, the sequence strictly decreases and eventually hits a prime or square of prime, so finite. (2) If v2(a1)=1 and 5∤a1, then a1 is a fixed point. (3) If v2(a1)≥2, then the recursion reduces to f(N)=13N/12 until the number is no longer divisible by 12. For the sequence to be infinite, the number after that chain must be a fixed point, leading to the conditions. (4) Sufficiency is shown by reversing the argument. The necessity uses the fact that if the sequence is infinite, it must eventually become a fixed point (since otherwise it would decrease to a prime). The only subtle point is the handling of numbers with exactly 4 divisors (like 6), but those are covered by the fixed point case. The proof is rigorous and covers all cases. No gaps remain.

We will determine all positive integers \(a_1\) for which the defined infinite sequence exists (each term has at least three proper divisors). Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). The recursion is \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\).

First, note that a number with fewer than three proper divisors cannot appear in the sequence, because then the next term would be undefined. Hence every \(a_n\) must be composite with at least three proper divisors. The smallest such number is \(6\) (proper divisors \(1,2,3\)). Numbers with exactly one proper divisor are the primes; with exactly two proper divisors are the squares of primes (e.g., \(4,9,25,\dots\)). So all \(a_n\) are in the set \(\mathcal{T}=\{N\ge 6\mid d(N)\ge 4\}\).

**Lemma 1.** If \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then the sequence is finite.  
*Proof.* Assume \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\). Then its smallest prime factor \(p\ge 5\). For any \(N\) with smallest prime factor \(p\ge 5\), the three largest proper divisors are \(N/p\), \(N/q\), \(1\) (if \(N\) has exactly two prime factors) or \(N/p\), \(N/q\), \(N/r\) (if \(N\) has at least three prime factors), where \(q,r\) are the next smallest primes. In either case \(f(N)<N\). Moreover \(f(N)\) is still a composite number with at least three proper divisors unless it becomes prime or a square of a prime. Since the sequence strictly decreases and is bounded below, it must eventually reach a number that has fewer than three proper divisors (otherwise it would be infinite and strictly decreasing, impossible). Hence the sequence cannot be infinite. ∎

Therefore \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\). Write \(a_1=6M\) with \(M\in\mathbb{N}\).

**Lemma 2.** If \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) and \(v_2(a_1)=1\) (i.e., exactly one factor of \(2\)) and \(5\nmid a_1\), then \(a_1\) is a fixed point of \(f\) and the sequence is constant.  
*Proof.* Since \(a_1\) is divisible by \(6\) and \(v_2=1\), the three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2\), \(3\), and \(6\). Hence \(f(a_1)=a_1/2+a_1/3+a_1/6=a_1\). Thus \(a_2=a_1\), and by induction all terms equal \(a_1\). The condition \(5\nmid a_1\) is needed because if \(5\mid a_1\) then the third smallest divisor is \(5\) instead of \(6\), and \(f(a_1)=31a_1/30\neq a_1\); a direct check shows such numbers lead to a prime (e.g., \(30\to31\)). So the condition is necessary for the fixed point. ∎

Now consider the case \(v_2(a_1)\ge 2\). Then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(12\). For any \(N\) with \(v_2(N)\ge 2\), the three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2\), \(3\), and \(4\) (since \(4\mid N\) and \(4<5\), \(4<6\)). Hence \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12\). Thus
\[
a_2 = \frac{13}{12}a_1.
\]
If \(a_2\) is still divisible by \(12\), we can apply the same formula again; otherwise the dynamics change. Let \(t=v_{12}(a_1)\) be the largest integer such that \(12^t\mid a_1\). Then \(t=\min\bigl(\lfloor v_2(a_1)/2\rfloor,\; v_3(a_1)\bigr)\). After \(t\) steps we obtain
\[
a_{t+1}=13^t\cdot u,\qquad u=\frac{a_1}{12^t}.
\]
Because \(12^t\mid a_1\), we have \(v_2(a_1)\ge 2t\) and \(v_3(a_1)\ge t\).

**Lemma 3.** If the sequence is infinite, then \(a_1\) must satisfy \(v_2(a_1)\) odd, \(v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}\), and \(5\nmid a_1\).  
*Proof.* The sequence is infinite, so all terms are in \(\mathcal{T}\). In particular \(a_{t+1}\) must be a fixed point (otherwise the sequence would eventually decrease to a prime). For \(a_{t+1}=13^t u\), we have \(v_2(a_{t+1})=v_2(u)\) and \(v_3(a_{t+1})=v_3(u)\) because \(13\) is odd and not \(3\). To have \(a_{t+1}\) be a fixed point we need \(v_2(a_{t+1})=1\) and \(5\nmid a_{t+1}\) (and \(v_3(a_{t+1})\ge1\)). Thus \(v_2(u)=1\) and \(5\nmid u\). Since \(u=a_1/12^t\) and \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(5\), \(v_5(a_1)=0\) implies \(5\nmid u\). Hence \(v_2(a_1)=2t+1\) (odd). Also \(v_3(a_{t+1})=v_3(u)=v_3(a_1)-t\ge1\), so \(v_3(a_1)\ge t+1 = \frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}\). ∎

**Lemma 4.** If \(a_1\) satisfies \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\), \(5\nmid a_1\), \(v_2(a_1)\) odd, and \(v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}\), then the sequence is infinite.  
*Proof.* Write \(a_1=2^{2k+1}\cdot3^{c}\cdot u\), where \(u\) is coprime to \(6\) and \(5\nmid u\). Since \(v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}=k+1\), we have \(c\ge k+1\). Then \(v_{12}(a_1)=\min(\lfloor v_2/2\rfloor, v_3)=\min(k, c)=k\) because \(c\ge k+1>k\). Hence \(t=k\). Then \(u'=a_1/12^k = 2^{2k+1-2k}\cdot3^{c-k}\cdot u = 2\cdot3^{c-k}\cdot u\). Since \(c-k\ge1\), \(u'\) is divisible by \(3\) and \(v_2(u')=1\). Also \(5\nmid u'\) because \(5\nmid a_1\). Therefore \(a_{k+1}=13^k\cdot u'\) has \(v_2=1\) and \(v_3\ge1\), so \(a_{k+1}\) is a fixed point of \(f\); consequently \(a_{n}=a_{k+1}\) for all \(n\ge k+1\). The sequence is constant and thus infinite. ∎

From Lemmas 1–4 we have characterized all possible \(a_1\). In terms of prime factorization:
\[
a_1 = 2^{2t+1}\cdot 3^{c}\cdot u,\qquad t\ge0,\; c\ge t+1,\; u\ge1,\; \gcd(u,6)=1,\; 5\nmid u.
\]
Equivalently, \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\), not divisible by \(5\), and the exponent of \(2\) is odd while the exponent of \(3\) is at least \((v_2(a_1)+1)/2\).

Thus the complete set of possible initial values is
\[
\boxed{\{a_1\in\mathbb{N}\mid a_1\equiv0\pmod6,\;5\nmid a_1,\;v_2(a_1)\text{ odd},\;v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}\}}.
\]
</solution>
